<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Western esotericism</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">"Arcane" and "Esoteric" redirect here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Arcane_(disambiguation)" title="Arcane (disambiguation)">Arcane (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Esoteric_(disambiguation)" title="Esoteric (disambiguation)">Esoteric (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tree_of_Life,_Medieval.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="357" data-file-width="260" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Tree_of_Life%2C_Medieval.jpg/220px-Tree_of_Life%2C_Medieval.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c3/Tree_of_Life%2C_Medieval.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tree_of_Life,_Medieval.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Tree_of_life_(Kabbalah)" title="Tree of life (Kabbalah)">tree of life as represented in the Kabbalah</a>, containing the <a href="/wiki/Sephirot" title="Sephirot">Sephiroth</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Western esotericism</b>, also called <b>esotericism</b> and <b>esoterism</b>, is a scholarly term for a wide range of loosely related unconventional ideas and movements which have developed within <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_society" title="Western society">Western society</a>. They are largely distinct from both orthodox <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Christian" title="Judeo-Christian">Judeo-Christian religion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a>. A trans-disciplinary field, esotericism has pervaded various forms of Western philosophy, religion, pseudoscience, art, literature, and music, continuing to affect intellectual ideas and popular culture.</p>
<p>The idea that a wide range of Western traditions and philosophies could be categorised together under the rubric that we now term "esotericism" developed in Europe during the late seventeenth century. The precise definition of Western esotericism has been debated by various academics, with a number of different options proposed. One scholarly model adopts its definition of "esotericism" from certain esotericist schools of thought themselves, treating "esotericism" as a <a href="/wiki/Perennial_philosophy" title="Perennial philosophy">perennialist</a> hidden, inner tradition. A second perspective argues that it is a category that encompasses world views which seek to embrace an 'enchanted' world view in the face of increasing de-enchantment. A third view, propounded by <a href="/wiki/Wouter_Hanegraaff" title="Wouter Hanegraaff">Wouter Hanegraaff</a>, views Western esotericism as a category encompassing all of Western culture's "rejected knowledge" that is accepted by neither the scientific establishment nor orthodox religious authorities.</p>
<p>The earliest traditions to later be labelled as forms of Western esotericism emerged in the Eastern Mediterranean during <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>, where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermetism" title="Hermetism">Hermetism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnosticism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a> developed as schools of thought distinct from mainstream Christianity. In <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> Europe, interest in many of these older ideas increased, with various intellectuals seeking to combine "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pagan" title="Pagan">pagan</a>" philosophies with the <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">Kabbalah</a> and Christian philosophy, resulting in the emergence of esoteric movements like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_theosophy" title="Christian theosophy">Christian theosophy</a>. The 17th century saw the development of initiatory societies professing esoteric knowledge such as <a href="/wiki/Rosicrucianism" title="Rosicrucianism">Rosicrucianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Freemasonry</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a> of the 18th century led to the development of new forms of esoteric thought. The 19th century saw the emergence of new trends of esoteric thought that have come to be known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occultism" title="Occultism">occultism</a>. Prominent groups in this century included the <a href="/wiki/Theosophical_Society" title="Theosophical Society">Theosophical Society</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hermetic_Order_of_the_Golden_Dawn" title="Hermetic Order of the Golden Dawn">Hermetic Order of the Golden Dawn</a>, which influenced the development of <a href="/wiki/Thelema" title="Thelema">Thelema</a>. <a href="/wiki/Modern_Paganism" title="Modern Paganism">Modern Paganism</a> also developed within occultism, and includes religious movements such as <a href="/wiki/Wicca" title="Wicca">Wicca</a>. Esoteric ideas permeated the <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">counterculture of the 1960s</a> and later cultural tendencies, from which emerged the <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a> movement in the 1970s.</p>
<p>Although the idea that these varying movements could be categorised together under the rubric of "Western esotericism" developed in the late 18th century, these esoteric currents were largely ignored as a subject of academic enquiry. The <a href="/wiki/Academic_study_of_Western_esotericism" title="Academic study of Western esotericism">academic study of Western esotericism</a> only emerged in the latter 20th century, pioneered by scholars like <a href="/wiki/Frances_Yates" title="Frances Yates">Frances Yates</a> and Faivre. There are now several peer-reviewed journals, university chairs, and academic societies devoted to this field. Esoteric ideas have meanwhile also exerted an influence in <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">popular culture</a>, appearing in art, literature, film, and music.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_esotericism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">adjective</a> "esoteric" first appeared in the second century CE as the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> term <i>esôterikós</i>, with the earliest known example of the word appearing in a satire authored by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucian_of_Samosata" title="Lucian of Samosata">Lucian of Samosata</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b80Hanegraaff2013a3_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b80Hanegraaff2013a3-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">noun</a> "esotericism", in its <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> form of "l'ésotérisme", was first used in 1828,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005a2_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005a2-2">[2]</a></sup> by Jacques Matter in his book, <i>Histoire du gnosticisme</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996384_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996384-3">[3]</a></sup> At this time it was being used in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a> and its critique of institutionalised religion, during which alternative religious groups began to disassociate themselves from the dominant <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> in Western Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b80_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b80-4">[4]</a></sup> During the nineteenth and twentieth centuries, the term "esotericism" came to commonly be seen as something that was distinct from Christianity, and which had formed a subculture that was at odds with the Christian mainstream from at least the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b80_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b80-4">[4]</a></sup> The term was popularized by the French occultist and ceremonial magician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eliphas_L%C3%A9vi" title="Eliphas Lévi">Eliphas Lévi</a> in the 1850s, and introduced into the English language by the Theosophist A. P. Sinnet in 1883.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996384_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996384-3">[3]</a></sup> Lévi also introduced the term <i>l'occultisme</i>, a notion that he developed against the background of contemporary socialist and Catholic discourses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrube2016aStrube2016b_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrube2016aStrube2016b-5">[5]</a></sup> "Esotericism" and "occultism" were often employed as synonyms until being distinguished by later scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996385_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996385-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conceptual_development">Conceptual development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_esotericism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Conceptual development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>'Western esotericism' is not a natural term but an artificial category, applied retrospectively to a range of currents and ideas that were known by other names at least prior to the end of the eighteenth century. [This] means that, originally, not all those currents and ideas were necessarily seen as belonging together:... it is only as recently as the later seventeenth century that we find the first attempts at presenting them as one single, coherent field or domain, and at explaining what they have in common. In short, 'Western esotericism' is a modern scholarly construct, not an autonomous tradition that already existed out there and merely needed to be discovered by historians.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">— The scholar of esotericism Wouter Hanegraaff, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a3_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a3-7">[7]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>The concept of "Western esotericism" is a modern scholarly construct rather than a pre-existing, self-defined tradition of thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b88Bogdan20076Hanegraaff2013a3_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b88Bogdan20076Hanegraaff2013a3-8">[8]</a></sup> In the late seventeenth century, several European Christian thinkers presented the argument that certain traditions of Western philosophy and thought could be categorised together, thus establishing the category that is now called "Western esotericism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff201278_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff201278-9">[9]</a></sup> The first to do so was <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ehregott_Daniel_Colberg&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ehregott Daniel Colberg (page does not exist)">Ehregott Daniel Colberg</a>, a German <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> who wrote <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Platonisch-Hermetisches_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Platonisch-Hermetisches Christianity (page does not exist)">Platonisch-Hermetisches Christianity</a></i> (1690–91). A hostile critic of various currents of Western thought that had emerged since the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> — among them <a href="/wiki/Paracelsianism" title="Paracelsianism">Paracelsianism</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Weigelianism&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Weigelianism (page does not exist)">Weigelianism</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_theosophy" title="Christian theosophy">Christian theosophy</a> — in his book he labelled all of these traditions under the category of "Platonic-Hermetic Christianity", arguing that they were <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heretical</a> to what he saw as true Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2012107_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2012107-10">[10]</a></sup> Despite his hostile attitude toward these traditions of thought, he was the first to connect these disparate philosophies and study them under one rubric, also recognising that these ideas linked back to earlier philosophies from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_antiquity" title="Late antiquity">late antiquity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2012107.E2.80.93108_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2012107.E2.80.93108-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Europe during the eighteenth century, amid the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>, these esoteric traditions came to be regularly categorised under the labels of "<a href="/wiki/Superstition" title="Superstition">superstition</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Magic_(paranormal)" title="Magic (paranormal)">magic</a>", and "the occult", terms which were often used interchangeably.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2012230_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2012230-12">[12]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academia" title="Academia">The modern academy</a>, which was then in the process of developing, consistently rejected and ignored topics coming under "the occult" and thus research into them was largely left to enthusiasts outside of academia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2012221_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2012221-13">[13]</a></sup> Indeed, according to the historian of esotericism <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wouter_J._Hanegraaff" title="Wouter J. Hanegraaff">Wouter J. Hanegraaff</a>, rejection of "occult" topics was seen as a "crucial identity marker" for any intellectuals seeking to affiliate themselves with the academy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2012221_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2012221-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Scholars established this category in the late 18th century after identifying "structural similarities" between "the ideas and world views of a wide variety of thinkers and movements" which prior to this had not been placed in the same analytical grouping.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a3_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a3-7">[7]</a></sup> According to the scholar of esotericism <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wouter_J._Hanegraaff" title="Wouter J. Hanegraaff">Wouter J. Hanegraaff</a>, the term provided a "useful generic label" for "a large and complicated group of historical phenomena that had long been perceived as sharing an <a href="/wiki/Family_resemblance" title="Family resemblance"><i>air de famille</i></a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996385_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996385-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Various academics have emphasised the idea that esotericism is a phenomenon unique to the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a>; as Faivre stated, an "empirical perspective" would hold that "esotericism is a Western notion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199417_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199417-14">[14]</a></sup> As scholars such as Faivre and Hanegraaff have pointed out, there is no comparable category of "Eastern" or "Oriental" esotericism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre19946Hanegraaff2013a14.E2.80.9315_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre19946Hanegraaff2013a14.E2.80.9315-15">[15]</a></sup> The emphasis on <i>Western</i> esotericism was nevertheless primarily devised to distinguish the field from a <i>universal</i> esotericism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsprem20148_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsprem20148-16">[16]</a></sup> Hanegraaff has characterised these as "recognisable world views and approaches to knowledge that have played an important although always controversial role in the history of Western culture."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a13_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a13-17">[17]</a></sup> Historian of religion Henrik Bogdan asserted that Western esotericism constituted "a third pillar of Western culture" alongside "doctrinal faith and rationality", being deemed heretical by the former and irrational by the latter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBogdan20077_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBogdan20077-18">[18]</a></sup> Scholars nevertheless recognise that various non-Western traditions have exerted "a profound influence" over Western esotericism, citing the prominent example of the <a href="/wiki/Theosophical_Society" title="Theosophical Society">Theosophical Society</a>'s incorporation of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a> concepts into its doctrines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBogdan2013177_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBogdan2013177-19">[19]</a></sup> Given these influences and the imprecise nature of the term "Western", the scholar of esotericism Kennet Granholm has argued that academics should cease referring to "<i>Western</i> esotericism" altogether, instead simply favouring "esotericism" as a descriptor of this phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGranholm2013a31.E2.80.9332_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGranholm2013a31.E2.80.9332-20">[20]</a></sup> This attitude was endorsed by Egil Asprem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsprem20144_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsprem20144-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_esotericism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The historian of esotericism <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Faivre" title="Antoine Faivre">Antoine Faivre</a> noted that "never a precise term, [esotericism] has begun to overflow its boundaries on all sides",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre19943_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre19943-22">[22]</a></sup> with both Faivre and Karen-Claire Voss stating that Western esotericism consists of "a vast spectrum of authors, trends, works of philosophy, religion, art, literature, and music".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivreVoss199548.E2.80.9349_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivreVoss199548.E2.80.9349-23">[23]</a></sup> There is broad agreement among scholars as to which currents of thought can be placed within a category of "esotericism", ranging from ancient <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnosticism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermetism" title="Hermetism">Hermetism</a> through to <a href="/wiki/Rosicrucianism" title="Rosicrucianism">Rosicrucianism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">Kabbalah</a> and on to more recent phenomenon such as the <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a> movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b79_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b79-24">[24]</a></sup> Nevertheless, "esotericism" itself remains a controversial term, with scholars specialising in the subject disagreeing as to how it can best be defined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b79_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b79-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Esotericism_as_a_universal.2C_secret.2C_inner_tradition">Esotericism as a universal, secret, inner tradition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_esotericism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Esotericism as a universal, secret, inner tradition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A definition adopted by some scholars has used "Western esotericism" in reference to "inner traditions" which are concerned with a "universal spiritual dimension of reality, as opposed to the merely external ('exoteric') religious institutions and dogmatic systems of established religions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a10.E2.80.9312_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a10.E2.80.9312-25">[25]</a></sup> According to this approach, "Western esotericism" is viewed as just one variant of a worldwide "esotericism" which can be found at the heart of all world religions and cultures, reflecting a hidden esoteric reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a11_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a11-26">[26]</a></sup> This usage of the term "esotericism" is closest to the original meaning of the word as it was used in late antiquity, where it was applied to secret spiritual teachings which were reserved for a specific elite and hidden from the masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a10_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a10-27">[27]</a></sup> This definition was popularised in the published work of nineteenth-century esotericists like <a href="/wiki/A._E._Waite" title="A. E. Waite">A. E. Waite</a>, who sought to combine their own mystical beliefs with a historical interpretation of esotericism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2012251_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2012251-28">[28]</a></sup> It subsequently became a popular approach within several esoteric movements, most notably <a href="/wiki/Martinism" title="Martinism">Martinism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Traditionalist_School" title="Traditionalist School">Traditionalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013b178_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013b178-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Universum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="770" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Universum.jpg/290px-Universum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Universum.jpg/435px-Universum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Universum.jpg/580px-Universum.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Universum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A colored version of the 1888 <a href="/wiki/Flammarion_engraving" title="Flammarion engraving">Flammarion engraving</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>This definition — originally developed by esotericists themselves — became popular among French academics during the 1980s, exerting a strong influence over the scholars <a href="/wiki/Mircea_Eliade" title="Mircea Eliade">Mircea Eliade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Corbin" title="Henry Corbin">Henry Corbin</a>, and the early work of Faivre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013b178_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013b178-29">[29]</a></sup> Within the academic field of <a href="/wiki/Religious_studies" title="Religious studies">religious studies</a>, those who study different religions in search of an inner, universal dimension to them all are termed "religionists".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a11_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a11-26">[26]</a></sup> Such religionist ideas also exerted an influence on more recent scholars like <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Goodrick-Clarke" title="Nicholas Goodrick-Clarke">Nicholas Goodrick-Clarke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Versluis" title="Arthur Versluis">Arthur Versluis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a11_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a11-26">[26]</a></sup> Versluis for instance defined "Western esotericism" as "inner or hidden spiritual knowledge transmitted through Western European historical currents that in turn feed into North American and other non-European settings".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVersluis20071_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVersluis20071-30">[30]</a></sup> He added that these Western esoteric currents all shared a core characteristic, "a claim to gnosis, or direct spiritual insight into cosmology or spiritual insight",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVersluis20071_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVersluis20071-30">[30]</a></sup> and accordingly he suggested that these currents could be referred to as "Western gnostic" just as much as "Western esoteric".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVersluis20072_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVersluis20072-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are various problems with this model for understanding Western esotericism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a11_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a11-26">[26]</a></sup> The most significant is that is rests upon the conviction that there really is a "universal, hidden, esoteric dimension of reality" that objectively exists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a11_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a11-26">[26]</a></sup> The existence of this universal inner tradition has not been discovered through scientific or scholarly enquiry; this had led some to claim that it does not exist, although Hanegraaff thought it better to adopt a view based in methodological agnosticism by stating that "we simply do not know - and cannot know" if it exists or not. He noted that, even if such a true and absolute nature of reality really existed, it would only be accessible through 'esoteric' spiritual practices, and could not be discovered or measured by the 'exoteric' tools of scientific and scholarly enquiry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a11.E2.80.9312_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a11.E2.80.9312-32">[32]</a></sup> Hanegraaff also highlighted that an attitude which seeks to uncover an inner hidden core of all esoteric currents masks the fact that such groups often contain significant differences from one another, being rooted in their own historical and social contexts, and expressing ideas and agendas which are mutually exclusive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a12_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a12-33">[33]</a></sup> A third issue was that many of those currents widely recognised as esoteric never concealed their teachings, and in the twentieth century came to permeate popular culture, thus problematizing the claim that esotericism could be defined by its hidden and secretive nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996385Von_Stuckrad2005b81_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996385Von_Stuckrad2005b81-34">[34]</a></sup> Moreover, Hanegraaff noted that when scholars adopt this definition, it shows that they subscribe to the religious doctrines which are espoused by the very groups that they are studying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996385_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff1996385-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Esotericism_as_an_enchanted_world_view">Esotericism as an enchanted world view</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_esotericism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Esotericism as an enchanted world view">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RWS_Tarot_01_Magician.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="528" data-file-width="300" height="387" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/de/RWS_Tarot_01_Magician.jpg/220px-RWS_Tarot_01_Magician.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/de/RWS_Tarot_01_Magician.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RWS_Tarot_01_Magician.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Magician_(Tarot)" title="The Magician (Tarot)">The Magician</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Tarot" title="Tarot">tarot</a> card displaying the Hermetic concept of "as above, so below." Faivre connected this concept to 'correspondences', his first defining characteristic of esotericism</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another approach to Western esotericism has treated it as a world view that embraces 'enchantment' in contrast to world views influenced by post-<a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">Cartesian</a>, post-<a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newtonian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Positivism" title="Positivism">positivist science</a> which have sought to '<a href="/wiki/Disenchantment" title="Disenchantment">dis-enchant</a>' the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a5_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a5-35">[35]</a></sup> Esotericism is therefore understood as comprising those world views which eschew a belief in instrumental <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">causality</a> and instead adopt a belief that all parts of the universe are interrelated without a need for causal chains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a5_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a5-35">[35]</a></sup> It therefore stands as a radical alternative to the disenchanted world views which have dominated Western culture since the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_revolution" title="Scientific revolution">scientific revolution</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a5_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a5-35">[35]</a></sup> and must therefore always be at odds with <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secular</a> culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a7_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a7-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>An early exponent of this definition was the historian of Renaissance thought <a href="/wiki/Frances_Yates" title="Frances Yates">Frances Yates</a> in her discussions of a "Hermetic Tradition", which she saw as an 'enchanted' alternative to established religion and rationalistic science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a6.E2.80.937_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a6.E2.80.937-37">[37]</a></sup> However, the primary exponent of this view was Faivre, who published a series of criteria for how to define "Western esotericism" in 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005a3Bogdan200710Hanegraaff2013a3.E2.80.934_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005a3Bogdan200710Hanegraaff2013a3.E2.80.934-38">[38]</a></sup> Faivre claimed that esotericism was "identifiable by the presence of six fundamental characteristics or components", four of which were "intrinsic" and thus vital to defining something as being esoteric, while the other two were "secondary" and thus not necessarily present in every form of esotericism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199410Von_Stuckrad2005a4Bergunder201014Hanegraaff2013a3_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199410Von_Stuckrad2005a4Bergunder201014Hanegraaff2013a3-39">[39]</a></sup> He listed these characteristics as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>"Correspondences": This is the idea that there are both real and symbolic correspondences existing between all things within the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199410Hanegraaff1996398Von_Stuckrad2005a4Versluis20077_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199410Hanegraaff1996398Von_Stuckrad2005a4Versluis20077-40">[40]</a></sup> As examples for this, Faivre pointed to the esoteric concept of the <a href="/wiki/Macrocosm_and_microcosm" title="Macrocosm and microcosm">macrocosm and microcosm</a>, often presented as the dictum of "as above, so below", as well as the astrological idea that the actions of the planets have a direct corresponding influence on the behaviour of human beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199410.E2.80.9311_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199410.E2.80.9311-41">[41]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Living Nature": Faivre argued that all esotericists envision the natural universe as being imbued with its own life force, and that as such they understand it as being "complex, plural, hierarchical".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199411Hanegraaff1996398Von_Stuckrad2005a4Versluis20077_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199411Hanegraaff1996398Von_Stuckrad2005a4Versluis20077-42">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Imagination and Mediations": Faivre believed that all esotericists place great emphasis on both the human <a href="/wiki/Imagination" title="Imagination">imagination</a>, and mediations – "such as rituals, symbolic images, mandalas, intermediary spirits" – as tools that provide access to worlds and levels of reality existing between the material world and the divine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199412Hanegraaff1996398.E2.80.93399Von_Stuckrad2005a4Versluis20077_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199412Hanegraaff1996398.E2.80.93399Von_Stuckrad2005a4Versluis20077-43">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Experience of Transmutation": Faivre's fourth intrinsic characteristic of esotericism was the emphasis that esotericists place on fundamentally transforming themselves through their practice, for instance through the spiritual transformation that it alleged to accompany the attainment of <a href="/wiki/Gnosis" title="Gnosis">gnosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199413Hanegraaff1996399.E2.80.93340Von_Stuckrad2005a4Versluis20077_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199413Hanegraaff1996399.E2.80.93340Von_Stuckrad2005a4Versluis20077-44">[44]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Practice of Concordance": The first of Faivre's secondary characteristics of esotericism was the belief – held by many esotericists, such as those in the <a href="/wiki/Traditionalist_School" title="Traditionalist School">Traditionalist School</a> – that there is a fundamental unifying principle or root from which all world religions and spiritual practices emerge. The common esoteric principle is that by attaining this unifying principle, the world's different beliefs can be brought together in unity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199414Hanegraaff1996400Von_Stuckrad2005a4Versluis20078_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199414Hanegraaff1996400Von_Stuckrad2005a4Versluis20078-45">[45]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Transmission": Faivre's second secondary characteristic was the emphasis on the transmission of esoteric teachings and secrets from a master to their discipline, through a process of <a href="/wiki/Initiation" title="Initiation">initiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199414.E2.80.9315Hanegraaff1996400Von_Stuckrad2005a4Versluis20078_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199414.E2.80.9315Hanegraaff1996400Von_Stuckrad2005a4Versluis20078-46">[46]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>Faivre's form of categorisation has been endorsed by scholars like Goodrick-Clarke,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodrick-Clarke20087.E2.80.9310_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodrick-Clarke20087.E2.80.9310-47">[47]</a></sup> and by 2007 Bogdan could note that Faivre's had become "the standard definition" of Western esotericism in use among scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBogdan200710_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBogdan200710-48">[48]</a></sup> However, in 2013 the scholar Kennet Granholm stated only that Faivre's definition had been "the dominating paradigm for a long while" and that it "still exerts influence among scholars outside the study of Western esotericism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGranholm2013b8_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGranholm2013b8-49">[49]</a></sup> The advantage of Faivre's system is that it allows varying esoteric traditions to be compared "with one another in a systematic fashion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005a4_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005a4-50">[50]</a></sup> However, criticisms have also been expressed of Faivre's theory, pointing out its various weaknesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005a5Hanegraaff2013a3_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005a5Hanegraaff2013a3-51">[51]</a></sup> Hanegraaff claimed that Faivre's approach entailed "reasoning by prototype" in that it relied upon already having a "best example" of what Western esotericism should look like, against which other phenomenon then had to be compared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a4.E2.80.935_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a4.E2.80.935-52">[52]</a></sup> The scholar of esotericism <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kocku_Von_Stuckrad&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kocku Von Stuckrad (page does not exist)">Kocku Von Stuckrad</a> noted that Faivre's taxonomy was based on his own areas of specialism – Renaissance Hermeticism, Christian Kabbalah, and Protestant Theosophy – and that it was thus not based on a wider understanding of esotericism as it has existed throughout history, from the ancient world to the contemporary period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005a5_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005a5-53">[53]</a></sup> Accordingly, Von Stuckrad suggested that it was a good typology for understanding "Christian esotericism in the early modern period" but lacked utility beyond that.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b83_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b83-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Esotericism_as_claims_to_higher_knowledge">Esotericism as claims to higher knowledge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_esotericism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Esotericism as claims to higher knowledge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>Somewhat crudely, esotericism can be described as a Western form of spirituality that stresses the importance of the individual effort to gain spiritual knowledge, or <i>gnosis</i>, whereby man is confronted with the divine aspect of existence.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">— Historian of religion Henrik Bogdan, 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBogdan20075_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBogdan20075-55">[55]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>As an alternative to Faivre's framework, Von Stuckrad developed his own variant, although argued that this did not represent a "definition" but rather a "a framework of analysis" for scholarly usage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b93_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b93-56">[56]</a></sup> He stated that "on the most general level of analysis", esotericism represented "the claim of higher knowledge", a claim to possessing "wisdom that is superior to other interpretations of cosmos and history" and which serves as a "master key for answering all questions of humankind".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b88_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b88-57">[57]</a></sup> Accordingly, he believed that esoteric groups placed a great emphasis on secrecy, not because they were inherently rooted in elite groups but because the idea of concealed secrets that can be revealed was central to their discourse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b89_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b89-58">[58]</a></sup> Examining the means of accessing higher knowledge, he highlighted two themes that he believed could be found within esotericism, that of mediation through contact with non-human entities, and individual experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b91.E2.80.9392_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b91.E2.80.9392-59">[59]</a></sup> Accordingly, for Von Stuckrad, esotericism could be best understood as "a structural element of Western culture" rather than as a selection of different schools of thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b80_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVon_Stuckrad2005b80-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Esotericism_as_.22rejected_knowledge.22">Esotericism as "rejected knowledge"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_esotericism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title='Edit section: Esotericism as "rejected knowledge"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An additional definition was proposed by Hanegraaff, and holds that "Western esotericism" is a category representing "the academy's dustbin of rejected knowledge."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a13_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a13-17">[17]</a></sup> In this respect, it contains all of the theories and world views that have been rejected by the mainstream intellectual community because they do not accord with "normative conceptions of religion, rationality and science".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a13_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a13-17">[17]</a></sup> His approach is rooted within the field of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_ideas" title="History of ideas">history of ideas</a>, and stresses the role of change and transformation over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergunder201018_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergunder201018-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Goodrick-Clarke was critical of this approach, believing that it relegated Western esotericism to the position of "a casualty of positivist and materialist perspectives in the nineteenth-century" and thus reinforces the idea that Western esoteric traditions were of little historical importance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodrick-Clarke20084_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodrick-Clarke20084-61">[61]</a></sup> Bogdan similarly expressed concern regarding Hanegraaff's definition, believing that it made the category of Western esotericism "all inclusive" and thus analytically useless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBogdan200715_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBogdan200715-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_esotericism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Antiquity">Late Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_esotericism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Late Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HermesTrismegistusCauc.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="407" data-file-width="300" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/HermesTrismegistusCauc.jpg/200px-HermesTrismegistusCauc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/98/HermesTrismegistusCauc.jpg 1.5x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HermesTrismegistusCauc.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A later illustration of Hermes Trismegistus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The origins of Western esotericism are in the Hellenistic Eastern Mediterranean, then part of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, during <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>, a period encompassing the first centuries of the <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">Common Era</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodrick-Clarke20083.2C_15Hanegraaff2013a18_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodrick-Clarke20083.2C_15Hanegraaff2013a18-63">[63]</a></sup> This was a milieu in which there was a mix of religious and intellectual traditions from Greece, Egypt, the Levant, Babylon, and Persia, and in which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Globalisation" title="Globalisation">globalisation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urbanisation" title="Urbanisation">urbanisation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Multiculturalism" title="Multiculturalism">multiculturalism</a> were bringing about socio-cultural change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodrick-Clarke200813Hanegraaff2013a18_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodrick-Clarke200813Hanegraaff2013a18-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>One component of this was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermetism" title="Hermetism">Hermetism</a>, an Egyptian Hellenistic school of thought that takes its name from the legendary Egyptian wise man, <a href="/wiki/Hermes_Trismegistus" title="Hermes Trismegistus">Hermes Trismegistus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVersluis200724Goodrick-Clarke200816.E2.80.9320Hanegraaff2013a19_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVersluis200724Goodrick-Clarke200816.E2.80.9320Hanegraaff2013a19-65">[65]</a></sup> In the 2nd and 3rd centuries CE, a number of texts appeared which were attributed to Hermes Trismegistus, including the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corpus_Hermeticum" title="Corpus Hermeticum">Corpus Hermeticum</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Asclepius" title="Asclepius">Asclepius</a></i>, and the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Discourse_on_the_Eighth_and_Ninth" title="The Discourse on the Eighth and Ninth">The Discourse on the Eighth and Ninth</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodrick-Clarke200816.E2.80.9320Hanegraaff2013a19_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodrick-Clarke200816.E2.80.9320Hanegraaff2013a19-66">[66]</a></sup> Although it is still debated as to whether Hermetism was a purely literary phenomenon, or whether there were communities of practitioners who acted on these ideas, it has been established that these texts discuss the true nature of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>, emphasising that humans must transcend rational thought and worldly desires in order to find <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a> and be reborn into a spiritual body of immaterial light, thereby achieving spiritual unity with divinity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodrick-Clarke200816.E2.80.9320Hanegraaff2013a19_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodrick-Clarke200816.E2.80.9320Hanegraaff2013a19-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another tradition of esoteric thought in Late Antiquity was <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnosticism</a>, which had a complex relationship with <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>. Various Gnostic sects existed, and they broadly believed that the divine light had been imprisoned within the material world by a malevolent entity known as the <a href="/wiki/Demiurge" title="Demiurge">Demiurge</a>, who was served by demonic helpers, the <a href="/wiki/Archon_(Gnosticism)" title="Archon (Gnosticism)">Archons</a>. It was the Gnostic belief that humans, who were imbued with the divine light, should seek to attain <a href="/wiki/Gnosis" title="Gnosis">gnosis</a> and thus escape from the world of matter and rejoin the divine source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199453Goodrick-Clarke200827.E2.80.9329Hanegraaff2013a19.E2.80.9320_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199453Goodrick-Clarke200827.E2.80.9329Hanegraaff2013a19.E2.80.9320-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>A third form of esotericism in Late Antiquity was <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a>, a school of thought influenced by the ideas of the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>. Advocated by such figures as <a href="/wiki/Plotinus" title="Plotinus">Plotinus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Porphyry_(philosopher)" title="Porphyry (philosopher)">Porphyry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iamblichus" title="Iamblichus">Iamblichus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Proclus" title="Proclus">Proclus</a>, Neoplatonism held that the human soul had fallen from its divine origins into the material world, but that it could progress, through a number of hierarchical spheres of being, to return to its divine origins once more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199452Goodrick-Clarke200820.E2.80.9327_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199452Goodrick-Clarke200820.E2.80.9327-68">[68]</a></sup> The later Neoplatonists performed <a href="/wiki/Theurgy" title="Theurgy">theurgy</a>, a ritual practice attested in such sources as the <i><a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Oracles" title="Chaldean Oracles">Chaldean Oracles</a></i>. Scholars are still unsure of precisely what theurgy involved, although it is known that it involved a practice designed to make gods appear, who could then raise the theurgist's mind to the reality of the divine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoodrick-Clarke200825Hanegraaff2013a20.E2.80.9321_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoodrick-Clarke200825Hanegraaff2013a20.E2.80.9321-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_esotericism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the fall of Rome, <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> and other aspects of the tradition were largely preserved in the Arab and Near Eastern world and introduced into Western Europe by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jew" title="Jew">Jews</a> and by the cultural contact between <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and south Italy. The 12th century saw the development of the <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">Kabbalah</a> in south Italy and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Spain" title="Medieval Spain">medieval Spain</a>. The medieval period also saw the publication of <a href="/wiki/Grimoire" title="Grimoire">grimoires</a> which offered often elaborate formulas for <a href="/wiki/Theurgy" title="Theurgy">theurgy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thaumaturgy" title="Thaumaturgy">thaumaturgy</a>. Many of the grimoires seem to have kabbalistic influence. Figures in alchemy from this period seem to also have authored or used grimoires.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Renaissance_and_Early_Modern_period">Renaissance and Early Modern period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_esotericism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Renaissance and Early Modern period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, a number of European thinkers began to synthesize "pagan" philosophies which were then being made available through Arabic translations with Christian thought and the Jewish <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">kabbalah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a25_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a25-70">[70]</a></sup> The earliest of these individuals was the Byzantine philosopher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plethon" title="Plethon">Plethon</a> (1355/60–1452?), who argued that the <i>Chaldean Oracles</i> represented an example of a superior religion of ancient humanity which had been passed down by the Platonists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a26_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a26-71">[71]</a></sup> Plethon's ideas interested the ruler of Florence, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosimo_de_Medici" title="Cosimo de Medici">Cosimo de Medici</a>, who employed Florentine thinker <a href="/wiki/Marsilio_Ficino" title="Marsilio Ficino">Marsilio Ficino</a> (1433–1499) to translate Plato's works into Latin. Ficino went on to translate and publish the works of various Platonic figures, arguing that their philosophies were compatible with Christianity, and allowing for the emergence of a wider movement in Renaissance Platonism, or Platonic Orientalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199458Hanegraaff2013a26.E2.80.9327_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199458Hanegraaff2013a26.E2.80.9327-72">[72]</a></sup> Ficino also translated part of the <i>Corpus Hermeticum</i>, although the rest would be translated by his contemporary, <a href="/wiki/Lodovico_Lazzarelli" title="Lodovico Lazzarelli">Lodovico Lazzarelli</a> (1447–1500).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a27_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a27-73">[73]</a></sup> Another core figure in this intellectual milieu was <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pico_della_Mirandola" title="Giovanni Pico della Mirandola">Giovanni Pico della Mirandola</a> (1463–1494), who achieved notability in 1486 by inviting scholars from across Europe to come and debate the 900 theses that he had written with him. Mirandola argued that all of these philosophies reflected a grand universal wisdom, however <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_VIII" title="Pope Innocent VIII">Pope Innocent VIII</a> condemned these actions, criticising him for attempting to mix pagan and Jewish ideas with Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a27.E2.80.9328_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a27.E2.80.9328-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pico's increased interest in Jewish kabbalah led to his development of a distinct form of <a href="/wiki/Christian_Kabbalah" title="Christian Kabbalah">Christian Kabbalah</a>. His work was built on by the German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johannes_Reuchlin" title="Johannes Reuchlin">Johannes Reuchlin</a> (1455–1522) who authored a prominent text on the subject, <i><a href="/wiki/De_Arte_Cabbalistica" title="De Arte Cabbalistica">De Arte Cabbalistica</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a28.E2.80.9329_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a28.E2.80.9329-75">[75]</a></sup> Christian Kabbalah was expanded in the work of the German <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Cornelius_Agrippa" title="Heinrich Cornelius Agrippa">Heinrich Cornelius Agrippa</a> (1486–1535/36), who used it as a framework through which to explore the philosophical and scientific traditions of Antiquity in his work <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_occulta_philosophia_libri_tres" title="De occulta philosophia libri tres">De occulta philosophia libri tres</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a29_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a29-76">[76]</a></sup> The work of Agrippa and other esoteric philosophers had been based in a pre-Copernican worldview, but following the arguments of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copernicus" title="Copernicus">Copernicus</a>, a more accurate understanding of the cosmos was established. Copernicus' theories were adopted into esoteric strains of thought by <a href="/wiki/Giordano_Bruno" title="Giordano Bruno">Giordano Bruno</a> (1548–1600), whose ideas would be deemed <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a> by the Roman Catholic Church, eventually resulting in his public execution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a30_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a30-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Square_compasses.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="852" data-file-width="808" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Square_compasses.svg/200px-Square_compasses.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Square_compasses.svg/300px-Square_compasses.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Square_compasses.svg/400px-Square_compasses.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Square_compasses.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Masonic <a href="/wiki/Square_and_Compasses" title="Square and Compasses">Square and Compasses</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A distinct strain of esoteric thought developed in Germany, where it came to be known as <i><a href="/wiki/Naturphilosophie" title="Naturphilosophie">Naturphilosophie</a></i>; although influenced by traditions from Late Antiquity and Medieval Kabbalah, it only acknowledged two main sources of authority: Biblical scripture and the natural world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a31_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a31-78">[78]</a></sup> The primary exponent of this approach was <a href="/wiki/Paracelsus" title="Paracelsus">Paracelsus</a> (1493/94–1541), who took inspiration from alchemy and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folk_magic" title="Folk magic">folk magic</a> to argue against the mainstream medical establishment, which based its approach on the ideas of <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>. Instead, Paracelsus urged doctors to learn medicine through an observation of the natural world, although in later work he also began to focus on overtly religious questions. His work would gain significant support in both areas over the following centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199461.E2.80.9363Hanegraaff2013a30.E2.80.9331_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199461.E2.80.9363Hanegraaff2013a30.E2.80.9331-79">[79]</a></sup> One of those influenced by Paracelsus was German cobbler <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacob_B%C3%B6hme" title="Jacob Böhme">Jacob Böhme</a> (1575–1624), who sparked the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_theosophy" title="Christian theosophy">Christian theosophy</a> movement through his attempts to solve the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_evil" title="Problem of evil">problem of evil</a>. Böhme argued that God had been created out of an unfathomable mystery, the <i>Ungrud</i>, and that God himself composed of a wrathful core, surrounded by the forces of light and love.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199463.E2.80.9364Hanegraaff2013a32_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199463.E2.80.9364Hanegraaff2013a32-80">[80]</a></sup> Although condemned by Germany's <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> authorities, Böhme's ideas spread and formed the basis for a number of small religious communities, such as <a href="/wiki/Johann_Georg_Gichtel" title="Johann Georg Gichtel">Johann Georg Gichtel</a>'s <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Angelic_Brethren&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Angelic Brethren (page does not exist)">Angelic Brethren</a> in <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Pordage" title="John Pordage">John Pordage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jane_Leade" title="Jane Leade">Jane Leade</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philadelphian_Society" title="Philadelphian Society">Philadelphian Society</a> in England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a32.E2.80.9333_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a32.E2.80.9333-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 1614 to 1616, the three <i><a href="/wiki/Rosicrucian_Manifestos" title="Rosicrucian Manifestos">Rosicrucian Manifestos</a></i> were published in Germany; these texts purporting to represent a secret initiatory brotherhood which had been founded centuries before by a German adept named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Rosenkreutz" title="Christian Rosenkreutz">Christian Rosenkreutz</a>. There is no evidence that Rosenkreutz was a genuine historical figure, or that a Rosicrucian Order had ever existed, and instead the manifestos are likely literary creations of Lutheran theologian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johann_Valentin_Andreae" title="Johann Valentin Andreae">Johann Valentin Andreae</a> (1586–1654). However, they inspired much public interest, with various individuals coming to describe themselves as "Rosicrucian" and claiming that they had access to secret esoteric knowledge as a result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199464.E2.80.9366Hanegraaff2013a33.E2.80.9334_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199464.E2.80.9366Hanegraaff2013a33.E2.80.9334-82">[82]</a></sup> A real iniatory brotherhood was established in late 16th-century Scotland through the transformation of Medieval stonemason guilds to include non-craftsman: <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Freemasonry</a>. Soon spreading into other parts of Europe, in England it largely rejected its esoteric character and embraced humanism and rationalism, while in France it embraced new esoteric concepts, particularly those from Christian theosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a35.E2.80.9336_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a35.E2.80.9336-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="18th.2C_19th_and_early_20th_centuries">18th, 19th and early 20th centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_esotericism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 18th, 19th and early 20th centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hypnotisk_seans_av_Richard_Bergh_1887.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1543" data-file-width="2004" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Hypnotisk_seans_av_Richard_Bergh_1887.jpg/220px-Hypnotisk_seans_av_Richard_Bergh_1887.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Hypnotisk_seans_av_Richard_Bergh_1887.jpg/330px-Hypnotisk_seans_av_Richard_Bergh_1887.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Hypnotisk_seans_av_Richard_Bergh_1887.jpg/440px-Hypnotisk_seans_av_Richard_Bergh_1887.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hypnotisk_seans_av_Richard_Bergh_1887.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Hypnotic séance</i>. Painting by Swedish artist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bergh" title="Richard Bergh">Richard Bergh</a>, 1887</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a> witnessed a process of increasing secularisation of European governments and an embrace of modern science and rationality within intellectual circles. In turn, a "modernist occult" emerged that reflected varied ways in which esoteric thinkers came to terms with these developments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a36_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a36-84">[84]</a></sup> One of the most prominent esotericists of this period was the Swedish naturalist <a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Swedenborg" title="Emanuel Swedenborg">Emanuel Swedenborg</a> (1688–1772), who attempted to reconcile science and religion after experiencing a vision of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a>. His writings focused on his visionary travels to heaven and hell and his communications with angels, claiming that the visible, materialist world parallels an invisible spiritual world, with correspondences between the two that do not reflect causal relations. Following his death, followers would found the Swedenborgian <a href="/wiki/The_New_Church" title="The New Church">New Church</a>, although his writings would influence a far wider array of esoteric philosophies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199472Hanegraaff2013a37_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199472Hanegraaff2013a37-85">[85]</a></sup> Another major figure within the esoteric movement of this period was the German physician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franz_Anton_Mesmer" title="Franz Anton Mesmer">Franz Anton Mesmer</a> (1734–1814), who developed the theory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_Magnetism" title="Animal Magnetism">Animal Magnetism</a>, which later came to be known more commonly as "Mesmerism". Mesmer claimed that a universal life force permeated everything, including the human body, and that illnesses were caused by a disturbance or block in this force's flow; he developed techniques which he claimed cleansed such blockages and restored the patient to full health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199476.E2.80.9377Hanegraaff2013a37.E2.80.9338_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199476.E2.80.9377Hanegraaff2013a37.E2.80.9338-86">[86]</a></sup> One of Mesmer's followers, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marquis_de_Puys%C3%A9gur" title="Marquis de Puységur">Marquis de Puységur</a>, discovered that mesmeric treatment could induce a state of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somnambulism" title="Somnambulism">somnumbulic</a> trance in which they claimed to enter visionary states and communicate with spirit beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a38_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a38-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>These somnumbulic trance-states would heavily influence the esoteric religion of <a href="/wiki/Spiritualism" title="Spiritualism">Spiritualism</a>, which emerged from the United States in the 1840s and spread throughout North American and Europe. Spiritualism was based on the concept that individuals could communicate with spirits of the deceased during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%C3%A9ances" title="Séances">séances</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199487Hanegraaff201338_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199487Hanegraaff201338-88">[88]</a></sup> Although most forms of Spiritualism had little theoretical depth, being largely practical affairs, full theological worldviews based on the movement would be articulated by <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson_Davis" title="Andrew Jackson Davis">Andrew Jackson Davis</a> (1826–1910) and <a href="/wiki/Allan_Kardec" title="Allan Kardec">Allan Kardec</a> (1804–1869).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a38_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a38-87">[87]</a></sup> Scientific interest in the claims of Spiritualism resulted in the development of the field of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychical_research" title="Psychical research">psychical research</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a38_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a38-87">[87]</a></sup> Somnambulism also exerted a strong influence on the early disciplines of <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychiatry" title="Psychiatry">psychiatry</a>; esoteric ideas purvey the work of many early figures in this field, most notably <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carl_Gustav_Jung" title="Carl Gustav Jung">Carl Gustav Jung</a>, although with the rise of <a href="/wiki/Psychoanalysis" title="Psychoanalysis">psychoanalysis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Behaviourism" title="Behaviourism">behaviourism</a> in the 20th century, these disciplines distanced themselves from esotericism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a38.E2.80.9339_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a38.E2.80.9339-89">[89]</a></sup> Also influenced by artificial somnambulism was the religion of <a href="/wiki/New_Thought" title="New Thought">New Thought</a>, founded by the American Mesmerist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phineas_P._Quimby" title="Phineas P. Quimby">Phineas P. Quimby</a> (1802–1866) and which revolved around the concept of "<a href="/wiki/Mind_over_matter" title="Mind over matter">mind over matter</a>", believing that illness and other negative conditions could be cured through the power of belief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a39_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a39-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pentagram_(Levi).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="612" data-file-width="612" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Pentagram_%28Levi%29.jpg/220px-Pentagram_%28Levi%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Pentagram_%28Levi%29.jpg/330px-Pentagram_%28Levi%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Pentagram_%28Levi%29.jpg/440px-Pentagram_%28Levi%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pentagram_(Levi).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pentagram of Eliphas Levi</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In Europe, a movement usually termed "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occultism" title="Occultism">occultism</a>" emerged as various figures attempted to find a "third way" between Christianity and positivist science while building on the ancient, medieval, and Renaissance traditions of esoteric thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a39_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a39-90">[90]</a></sup> In France, following the social upheaval of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1789_Revolution" title="1789 Revolution">1789 Revolution</a>, various figures emerged in this occultist milieu who were heavily influenced by traditional Catholicism, the most notable of whom were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eliphas_L%C3%A9vi" title="Eliphas Lévi">Eliphas Lévi</a> (1810–1875) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papus" title="Papus">Papus</a> (1865–1916).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrube2016aHanegraaff2013a_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrube2016aHanegraaff2013a-91">[91]</a></sup> Also significant was <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Gu%C3%A9non" title="René Guénon">René Guénon</a> (1886–1951), whose concern with tradition led him to develop an occult viewpoint termed <a href="/wiki/Traditionalist_School" title="Traditionalist School">Traditionalism</a>; it espoused the idea of an original, universal tradition, and thus a rejection of <a href="/wiki/Modernity" title="Modernity">modernity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a40_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a40-92">[92]</a></sup> His Traditionalist ideas would have a strong influence on later esotericists like <a href="/wiki/Julius_Evola" title="Julius Evola">Julius Evola</a> (1898–1974) and <a href="/wiki/Frithjof_Schuon" title="Frithjof Schuon">Frithjof Schuon</a> (1907–1998).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a40_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a40-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the Anglophone world, the burgeoning occult movement owed more to <a href="/wiki/Libertine" title="Libertine">Enlightenment libertines</a>, and thus was more often of an anti-Christian bent that saw wisdom as emanating from the pre-Christian pagan religions of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a40_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a40-92">[92]</a></sup> Various Spiritualist mediums came to be disillusioned with the esoteric thought available, and sought inspiration in pre-Swedenborgian currents; the most prominent of these were <a href="/wiki/Emma_Hardinge_Britten" title="Emma Hardinge Britten">Emma Hardinge Britten</a> (1823–1899) and <a href="/wiki/Helena_Blavatsky" title="Helena Blavatsky">Helena Blavatsky</a> (1831–1891), the latter of whom called for the revival of the "occult science" of the ancients, which could be found in both the East and West. Authoring the influential <i><a href="/wiki/Isis_Unveiled" title="Isis Unveiled">Isis Unveiled</a></i> (1877) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_Doctrine" title="The Secret Doctrine">The Secret Doctrine</a></i> (1888), she co-founded the <a href="/wiki/Theosophical_Society" title="Theosophical Society">Theosophical Society</a> in 1875.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199493.E2.80.9394Hanegraaff2013a40.E2.80.9341_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199493.E2.80.9394Hanegraaff2013a40.E2.80.9341-93">[93]</a></sup> Subsequent leaders of the Society, namely <a href="/wiki/Annie_Besant" title="Annie Besant">Annie Besant</a> (1847–1933) and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Webster_Leadbeater" title="Charles Webster Leadbeater">Charles Webster Leadbeater</a> (1854–1934) interpreted modern theosophy as a form of ecumenical esoteric Christianity, resulting in their proclamation of Indian <a href="/wiki/Jiddu_Krishnamurti" title="Jiddu Krishnamurti">Jiddu Krishnamurti</a> (1895–1986) as world messiah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a41_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a41-94">[94]</a></sup> In rejection of this was the breakaway <a href="/wiki/Anthroposophical_Society" title="Anthroposophical Society">Anthroposophical Society</a> founded by <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Steiner" title="Rudolf Steiner">Rudolf Steiner</a> (1861–1925).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a41_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a41-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>New esoteric understandings of magic also developed in the latter part of the 19th century. One of the pioneers of this was American <a href="/wiki/Paschal_Beverly_Randolph" title="Paschal Beverly Randolph">Paschal Beverly Randolph</a> (1825–1875), who argued that sexual energy and psychoactive drugs could be used for magical purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a41_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a41-94">[94]</a></sup> In England, the <a href="/wiki/Hermetic_Order_of_the_Golden_Dawn" title="Hermetic Order of the Golden Dawn">Hermetic Order of the Golden Dawn</a>, an initiatory order devoted to magic which based itself on an understanding of kabbalah, was founded in the latter years of the century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaivre199491Hanegraaff2013a41_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaivre199491Hanegraaff2013a41-95">[95]</a></sup> One of the most prominent members of that order was <a href="/wiki/Aleister_Crowley" title="Aleister Crowley">Aleister Crowley</a> (1875–1947), who went on to proclaim the religion of <a href="/wiki/Thelema" title="Thelema">Thelema</a> and become a prominent member of the <a href="/wiki/Ordo_Templi_Orientis" title="Ordo Templi Orientis">Ordo Templi Orientis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a41.E2.80.9342_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a41.E2.80.9342-96">[96]</a></sup> Some of their contemporaries developed esoteric schools of thought that did not entail magic, namely the Greco-Armenian teacher <a href="/wiki/George_Gurdjieff" title="George Gurdjieff">George Gurdjieff</a> (1866–1949) and his Russian pupil <a href="/wiki/P._D._Ouspensky" title="P. D. Ouspensky">P. D. Ouspensky</a> (1878–1947).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a42_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHanegraaff2013a42-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Emergent occult and esoteric systems found increasing popularity in the early 20th century, especially in Western Europe. Occult lodges and secret societies flowered among European intellectuals of this era who had largely abandoned traditional forms of Christianity. The spreading of secret teachings and <a href="/wiki/Magic_(paranormal)" title="Magic (paranormal)">magic</a> practices found enthusiastic adherents in the chaos of Germany during the interwar years. Notable writers such as <a href="/wiki/Guido_von_List" title="Guido von List">Guido von List</a> spread neo-pagan, nationalist ideas, based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wotanism" title="Wotanism">Wotanism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">Kabbalah</a>. Many influential and wealthy Germans were drawn to secret societies such as the <a href="/wiki/Thule_Society" title="Thule Society">Thule Society</a>.
